<div id="review_1966570112" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1966570112" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1966570112?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571322862" href="/book/show/25320279-nightwood">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people interested in gay literature </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=gay-lit">gay-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=modernist-movement">modernist-movement</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=3-star-reads">3-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1966570112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16983900577054360750">It’s hard to believe what this book turned into as I got further in. In the preface, the book receives copious amounts of praise from Jeanette Winterson. She was influenced by the blatant lesbian content Barnes presents here: it encouraged her to display the same in her works. T.S Eliot even praised it, and T.S Eliot criticised everything to death. That first page will, nevertheless, always remain awful. But this is a book about appearances; it is a book about seeming rather than being, as the b</span>
  <span id="freeText16983900577054360750" style="display:none">It’s hard to believe what this book turned into as I got further in. In the preface, the book receives copious amounts of praise from Jeanette Winterson. She was influenced by the blatant lesbian content Barnes presents here: it encouraged her to display the same in her works. T.S Eliot even praised it, and T.S Eliot criticised everything to death. That first page will, nevertheless, always remain awful. But this is a book about appearances; it is a book about seeming rather than being, as the book progresses it does, indeed, change completely. The language became almost poetical and eloquent: <br /><br /><b>
  <i>“Those long remembered can alone claim to be long forgotten.”</i>
</b><br /><br />For me, this is a book that requires a completely new approach to reading. If you go into this expecting a linear progression or a sense of fulfilment, then you will be highly disappointed. This is something different, a piece of art that captures the intertwining lives of a bunch of people who are in themselves a little bit different. <br /><br />Centre to them is the character Robin. She has a strange effect on people; she possesses a power, an ability that draws people to her. A sense of strangeness or otherness that is hard to pinpoint, though it is one that makes people fall in love with her rather quickly. She is not a conventional person. Anyone who spends time with her drastically risks getting hurt. She moves on from lovers rather quickly but her partners most certainly do not move on from her, ever. Her existence is a strange one. I would argue that she’s not really living, but only existing. In this sense she reminded me very much of Andre Bretton's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/110457.Nadja?ac=1&amp;from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">Nadja</a><br /><br />She seems to float through life without a sense of purpose or direction. Instead she has this innate drive that she doesn’t quite understand. She is always drawn away from what she has. It’s almost like she harbours a perpetual sense of emptiness that she is longing to fill; thus, she goes roaming at night: she goes looking for something. In the romantic sense, this seems to be a communion with nature. The ending of the book could suggest that she finds it, but it could also suggest that she has gone completely insane due to her unfulfilling life. It’s also worth researching the author’s personal biography and its comparison with the lesbian elements of the story. It’s worth reading up on the see the parallels, and the shadows at the back of the book that suggest that this is, at least in part, semi-autobiographical. <br /><br /><i>“And must I, perchance, like careful writers, guard myself against the conclusions of my readers?” </i><br /><br /><i>Nightwood</i> is an unusual book, highly experimental even by the parameters of modernist literature. It is one that must be experienced and pondered. In a strange sort of way, it is more like poetry than a novel. The words are a vessel for capturing an essence of something meaningful. This is a book to be scrutinised and studied to see the depth of the work, but it is not one I overly enjoyed reading. A patient reader is required.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16983900577054360750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12092929969290999964">Fourth reading, and it remains just as much a mystery as ever. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/82947.Marianne_Moore' title='Marianne Moore' rel='nofollow noopener'>Marianne Moore</a> said that "reading Djuna Barnes is like reading a foreign language, which you understand," and while I don't disagree I find that any sensation of "comprehension" simply feels like entering another locked room to puzzle out of. A labyrinth with no exit, and I wouldn't have it any other way. [Apr 2017]<br/><br/>...<br/><br/><br/>After a second reading was compelled to include the missing fifth star. Maybe someday I'll be able to write something</span>
  <span id="freeText12092929969290999964" style="display:none">Fourth reading, and it remains just as much a mystery as ever. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/82947.Marianne_Moore" title="Marianne Moore" rel="nofollow noopener">Marianne Moore</a> said that "reading Djuna Barnes is like reading a foreign language, which you understand," and while I don't disagree I find that any sensation of "comprehension" simply feels like entering another locked room to puzzle out of. A labyrinth with no exit, and I wouldn't have it any other way. [Apr 2017]<br /><br />...<br /><br /><br />After a second reading was compelled to include the missing fifth star. Maybe someday I'll be able to write something that would do this magnificent, enigmatic text justice. [Mar 2014]<br /><br />...<br /><br /><br />So I'm not used to this kind of reaction with a book--finished it this morning, and I might very well start it all over again. Immediately. This <i>never</i> happens to me. <br /><br />And this despite not knowing what the hell was going on half (most?) of the time, but by the end I became intoxicated by the sheer absurdity that made me laugh stupidly despite being in public, the unexpected submersions into harrowing despair, the (to blatantly steal Ned Rorem's characterization) "gorgeous claustrophobia" the novel evokes. I've never come across anything quite like this before. <br /><br />Was initially intrigued by Susan Sontag's comment in her journal that her and her friends were essentially characters out of this novel; I wonder now how much of her own romantic sufferings were modeled off of Norah Flood's...<br /><br /><i>"'How do you stand it, then?' she demanded. 'How do you live at all if this wisdom of yours is not only the truth, but the price?'"</i> [Apr 2009]</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_63405698" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63405698" />
    <a title="Jimmy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1107219-jimmy"><img alt="Jimmy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582301234p2/1107219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63405698?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimmy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimmy" href="/user/show/1107219-jimmy">Jimmy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1107219-jimmy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63405698" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7759467199969823197">T.S. Elliot said of <i>Nightwood</i>, that it was "so good a novel that only sensibilities trained on poetry can wholly appreciate it". It's really more like a poetic dream than it is a novel. This isn't really because there is no narrative to be found, there is, and what's more, there is a clearly defined romantic conflict between the two main female characters, Nora Flood and Robin Vote. What makes it poetic is probably the flowery digressions that follow the brief explanations of what is happening i</span>
  <span id="freeText7759467199969823197" style="display:none">T.S. Elliot said of <i>Nightwood</i>, that it was "so good a novel that only sensibilities trained on poetry can wholly appreciate it". It's really more like a poetic dream than it is a novel. This isn't really because there is no narrative to be found, there is, and what's more, there is a clearly defined romantic conflict between the two main female characters, Nora Flood and Robin Vote. What makes it poetic is probably the flowery digressions that follow the brief explanations of what is happening in the story. These digressions are eclectic though, with a focus on a variety of themes; the war, Catholicism, Judaism, and the cultural qualities of several different nationalities. Although, the book's main focus lies in Nora Flood's complicated and, more or less, unrequited love for Robin Vote. <br /><br />To be honest, it seems as though one's opinions of <i>Nightwood</i> should be reserved for after a second reading. The opening introduces a world that goes through a number of alterations in tone and feeling. As the reader starts to pay attention to the story, Barnes' overwhelming flood of poetic language dominates the narrative, almost to the point where they are stunned by the beauty of the sentences and descriptions, not to mention the countless aphorisms spouted by Barnes' characters. Lines such as "To pay homage to our past is the only gesture that also includes the future." sort of tranquilized my mind. I found that I had stopped reading, glanced out of the window of the bus, and mused on the paradox of time for about five minutes. <br /><br />So, in short, it's a book of so many beautiful distractions that it's difficult to describe. What I've just read still eludes my mind to an extent, but whatever it was, I remember being moved by its poetic boldness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7759467199969823197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review63405698">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_2219495696" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2219495696" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2219495696?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=aere-perennius">aere-perennius</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2219495696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16186565296936573280"><i>“The unendurable is the beginning of the curve of joy.”</i><br/>- Djuna Barnes, Nightwood<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579319899i/28797285._SY540_.jpg' width='373' height='576' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I listened to this novel one night as I drove from Phoenix to Las Vegas. It was ominously dark, beautiful and creepy. I guess that equally applies to the book as to the drive. Art exists when something can be both creepy and beautiful at the same time. This isn't David Lynch, but I can imagine few other directors directing this book into a movie. Nightwood also gave my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13033.The_Alexandria_Quartet__The_Alexandria_Quartet__1_4_' title='The Alexandria Quartet (The Alexandria Quartet #1-4) by Lawrence Durrell' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Alexandria Quartet</a> vibes. Barnes like Durrel</span>
  <span id="freeText16186565296936573280" style="display:none"><i>“The unendurable is the beginning of the curve of joy.”</i><br>- Djuna Barnes, Nightwood<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579319899i/28797285._SY540_.jpg" width="373" height="576" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I listened to this novel one night as I drove from Phoenix to Las Vegas. It was ominously dark, beautiful and creepy. I guess that equally applies to the book as to the drive. Art exists when something can be both creepy and beautiful at the same time. This isn't David Lynch, but I can imagine few other directors directing this book into a movie. Nightwood also gave my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13033.The_Alexandria_Quartet__The_Alexandria_Quartet__1_4_" title="The Alexandria Quartet (The Alexandria Quartet #1-4) by Lawrence Durrell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Alexandria Quartet</a> vibes. Barnes like Durrell can capture the humanity of freaks and outcasts. She can disturb you and seduce you at the same time. I can see veins of Nightwood web through the later novels by Patricia Highsmith. As a CIS white male, reading books like Nightwood are useful. They give me a glimpse or shade of an experience that is completely foreign to mine. But, I'm not sure how far to extend that because at times, reading Nightwood felt like I was traveling through a nightmare drunk. I was disoriented, disturbed, and on shifting literary sand. But I have rarely read something that felt more like a trip.</span>

<div id="review_541327302" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/541327302?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/531153-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781564780805" href="/book/show/53103.Nightwood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/531153-jonathan?shelf=2015-the-modernists">2015-the-modernists</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer541327302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14699820733998736962">My second reading of this, but my first of the Dalkey edition. Reading it along with other of her work this year I have no doubt of her place amongst the great literary geniuses of the inter-war era. She is unafraid of complexity, subtlety and nuance. She is unabashedly, proudly, queer (and the un-censored Dalkey edition does much to bring the transgressive power of this text back to life). She has the intelligence, ambition and courage to produce truly great art.<br/><br/>This is one of the great books o</span>
  <span id="freeText14699820733998736962" style="display:none">My second reading of this, but my first of the Dalkey edition. Reading it along with other of her work this year I have no doubt of her place amongst the great literary geniuses of the inter-war era. She is unafraid of complexity, subtlety and nuance. She is unabashedly, proudly, queer (and the un-censored Dalkey edition does much to bring the transgressive power of this text back to life). She has the intelligence, ambition and courage to produce truly great art.<br /><br />This is one of the great books of the dark, of that which we have always already hidden. It is a great work of Modernism, and was recognised as such by at least one of the Trinity - as T.S.E's editing and introduction make clear, he certainly recognised the importance of the text, despite his over-eager use of the red pen...<br /><br />This book does not need a review from me, it has managed to remain above the earth and no spade-work is required. If you plan to read it, I would say that the only edition worth getting is the Dalkey and that you do the book and yourself a disservice by reading any other version...</span>

<div id="review_568906344" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/568906344" />
    <a title="Mala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5960716-mala"><img alt="Mala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435586901p2/5960716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/568906344?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mala" href="/user/show/5960716-mala">Mala</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Outcasts, Tortured souls</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=disturbing">disturbing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=american-writers">american-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=female-writer">female-writer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=dream-fantasy-horror">dream-fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=modern-fiction">modern-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer568906344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4191437637504799297"><br/><br/><b> Night people do not bury their dead, but on the neck of you, their beloved and waking, sling the creature, husked of its gestures. And where you go, it goes, the two of you, your living and her dead, that will not die; to daylight, to life, to grief, until both are carrion.</b><br/><br/>Nightwood is such a strange book and this isn't so much a ramrod- straight person's reaction to gay-lesbian literature as the feverish, dream-like quality of the text– like you've stumbled into someone's nightmare & can't fin</span>
  <span id="freeText4191437637504799297" style="display:none"><br /><br /><b> Night people do not bury their dead, but on the neck of you, their beloved and waking, sling the creature, husked of its gestures. And where you go, it goes, the two of you, your living and her dead, that will not die; to daylight, to life, to grief, until both are carrion.</b><br /><br />Nightwood is such a strange book and this isn't so much a ramrod- straight person's reaction to gay-lesbian literature as the feverish, dream-like quality of the text– like you've stumbled into someone's nightmare &amp; can't find a way out.<br /><br />It's a simple book, really- plotwise- a handful of characters- expats, exiles, outcasts living a rootless existence in Paris &amp; New York– "All the characters are exiles of one kind or another - Americans, Irish, Austrian, Jewish. This is the beginning of the modern diaspora - all peoples, all places, all change." The narrative also moves to Vienna &amp; Berlin but other than the seedy, underworld nightlife of Paris, of cheap bars &amp; prostitutes, the grand old mansions &amp; parks of Vienna- the 'action' mostly takes place through dialogues, by which the characters reveal themselves &amp; one another. Most important in this regard, is the character of Dr. Matthew O’Connor whose scathing, weary, penetrating monologues* form the bulk of this slender book &amp; foreshadow many happenings.<br /><br />I wisely used points from T.S.Eliot's introduction as signposts, that:<br /><br /><i>The book is not simply a collection of individual portraits; the characters are all knotted together, as people are in real life, by what we may call chance or destiny.</i><br /><br /><i>The book is not a psychopathic study. The miseries that people suffer through their particular abnormalities of temperament are visible on the surface: the deeper design is that of the human misery and bondage which is universal...To regard this group of people as a horrid sideshow of freaks is ...to miss the point.</i><br /><br />It's a deeply revealing psychological novel though, with surrealist tone where the Id is displayed on the pages for everyone to see– at the centre of this nightly world of secrets, shadows and sin- stands the beautiful, enigmatic, androgynous &amp; vulnerable Robin Vote– desired &amp; lusted after by her ex-husband Felix &amp; her lesbian lovers Nora &amp; Jenny. They want to know everything about her the way M.Swann wanted each &amp; every detail of Odette's life– to possess her soul as it were &amp; isn't something inherently evil about that, this desire for control, for possession? For our soul belongs with God &amp; the only person fighting for it is the devil.<br /><br />Religion is there in Nightwood in a big way– is it incidental that two important scenes involving Dr. Matthew &amp; Robin respectively take place in a church? Or that they are practising Catholics forever condemned to guilt for their sexuality which marginalises them?<br />The people in Nightwood are hurt &amp; hurting– I wondered if the passions played out on such a large tragic scale- such love, such devotion, such jealousy, such anguish, cause the desire was a forbidden one? The book was written in 1936 after all &amp; who can really say, even now, that alternative sexuality has found mainstream acceptance!?<br /><br /><u>A note on the ending*</u><br /><br />Nightwood's ending has puzzled &amp; baffled critcs &amp; readers alike: for character resolution though, the ending has psychological veracity– in her regression to animal-like state, Robin, achieves the primal state which her introduction had promised:<br /><br />She’s “a woman who is beast turning human,” “an infected carrier of the past," and her skin is “the texture of plant life."<br /><br />Many would like to see in her final defiance; her degradation– the erotic subtext of the final scene is hard to miss. I'm reminded of an incident when I was 13/14 yrs old, reading Manto's* short stories, in one of them, a woman jilted by her rich lover, sleeps with a diseased, filthy mongrel as an act of insult towards him. Now I had seen, in my friends' homes, their pet kittens lolling all over the bed so I thought maybe a dog too could sleep in the bed! I asked my mom to explain the story to me, needless to say I got a sound verbal thrashing &amp; was told to immediately return the book to the library &amp; not to go around asking neighbours the meaning of the story!<br />I wonder what mom would say now!<br /><br />(*)Not soliloquy or dialogue but monologue cause Mr.Eliot says so: "His monologues, brilliant and witty in themselves as they are, are not dictated by an indifference to other human beings, but on the contrary by a hypersensitive awareness of them."<br /><br />(**)Saadat Hasan Manto - Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia– an author worth reading.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saadat_Hasan_Manto">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saadat_H...</a><br /><br />Links for Nightwood:<br />I loved this article cause our thoughts went in the same directions:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://departments.knox.edu/engdept/commonroom/Volume13.2/Jake_Runge.html">http://departments.knox.edu/engdept/c...</a><br /><br />An interesting take on Robin's character &amp; the final scene.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://drhanan.com/papers/Robin&#39;s%20Silence.htm">http://drhanan.com/papers/Robin&#39;s...</a><br /><br />Creatures of the dark | Books | The Guardian<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.guardian.co.uk/books/2007/mar/31/featuresreviews.guardianreview32">http://www.guardian.co.uk/books/2007/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4191437637504799297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_428747999" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/428747999" />
    <a title="knig" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6163905-knig"><img alt="knig" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/428747999?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="knig" class="user" itemprop="url" name="knig" href="/user/show/6163905-knig">knig</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163905-knig?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163905-knig?shelf=blancmange-of-ideas">blancmange-of-ideas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer428747999" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14701462437504376287">Nightwood plays out lenticularly: Christ-cum Rasputin- like Dr O’Connor dominates the central frame with secondary characters phasing in and out in tune with a subtle rotational accretion of meditational ‘om’ spiked Eurekas.<br/><br/>A trifecta of bisexual women in perpetual locomotion seek out a Pythagorian articulation of their triangular ‘saltarello’, overseen by the gregarious doctor and overshadowed by a jilted husband. This then is the plot, what little of it there is.<br/><br/>Character driven in extremis, ‘</span>
  <span id="freeText14701462437504376287" style="display:none">Nightwood plays out lenticularly: Christ-cum Rasputin- like Dr O’Connor dominates the central frame with secondary characters phasing in and out in tune with a subtle rotational accretion of meditational ‘om’ spiked Eurekas.<br /><br />A trifecta of bisexual women in perpetual locomotion seek out a Pythagorian articulation of their triangular ‘saltarello’, overseen by the gregarious doctor and overshadowed by a jilted husband. This then is the plot, what little of it there is.<br /><br />Character driven in extremis, ‘Nightwood’ is more akin to a loose compilation of essays which would serve just as well as their own, independent exegis. Barnes unleashes a whirlwind of speculation, contemplation, meditation and probably other kinds of ‘ations’ I can’t think of now. Her thinking is original, her language saturated with unusual and lyrical associations. <br /><br />A tapestry of observations, then, on the compilations of living: with a rather umm, promiscuous extrapolation on the nature of ‘jewishness’ which most critics seem to gloss over, but which shocked me a little nonetheless. Of course its because she wrote this pre WWII. We don’t dare nowadays. Hell, we don’t dare much of anything nowadays: we’re too PCed (read as pissed).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14701462437504376287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1528769700" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1528769700" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1528769700?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780811200059" href="/book/show/537716.Nightwood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=u-s-lit">u-s-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1528769700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18172522383228017791">The reading gods have a lot of time on their hands. They conspire, they do. How else to explain that two of the last four books I've read were hi-jacked by characters who went on essentially book-length perorations. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/783505.Embers' title='Embers by Sándor Márai' rel='nofollow noopener'> Embers</a> by Sándor Márai, an old man invites a very old friend to dinner and then, for 120 pages, tells him the story the friend already knows. Here, in 'Nightwood', characters find themselves drawn to Matthew O'Connor, a cross-dressing, tortured alcoholic, playing at a doctor, who</span>
  <span id="freeText18172522383228017791" style="display:none">The reading gods have a lot of time on their hands. They conspire, they do. How else to explain that two of the last four books I've read were hi-jacked by characters who went on essentially book-length perorations. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/783505.Embers" title="Embers by Sándor Márai" rel="nofollow noopener"> Embers</a> by Sándor Márai, an old man invites a very old friend to dinner and then, for 120 pages, tells him the story the friend already knows. Here, in 'Nightwood', characters find themselves drawn to Matthew O'Connor, a cross-dressing, tortured alcoholic, playing at a doctor, who has both insight and inside scoop, and shovels it out, chapter after chapter, in a stream of consciousness fantasy.<br /><br />As you might imagine, some of this sounded like gibberish; lovely, but gibberish nonetheless:<br /><br /><i>Gurus, who, I trust you know, are Indian teachers, expect you to contemplate the acorn ten years at a stretch, and if, in that time, you are no wiser about the nut, you are not very bright, and that may be the only certainty with which you will come away, which is a post-graduate melancholy--for no man can find a greater truth than his kidney will allow.</i><br /><br />Or:<br /><br /><i>As for me, I tuck myself in at night, well content, because I am my own charlatan.</i><br /><br />And people accuse <i>me</i> of being enigmatic.<br /><br />O'Connor is composing a new song:<br /><br /><i>The song is entitled, 'Mother, put the wheel away, I cannot spin tonight.' Its other name, 'According to me, everyone is a kind-of-a-son-of-a-bitch,' to be sung to two octarinas and one concertina, and, ........</i><br /><br />....And if you wondered about racial notions in the 1930s, well, O'Connor shares. After some thought, and some erasures, I've decided not to.<br /><br />There are few interruptions. But Nora ....<br /><br /><i>Nora stood up, but she sat down again. "How do you stand it, then?" she demanded. "How do you live at all if this wisdom of yours is not only the truth, but also the price?"</i><br /><br />The author too, albeit in O'Connor's speech, intrudes: <i>And must I, perchance, like careful writers, guard myself against the conclusion of my reader?</i><br /><br />I appreciated, I liked, and I wearied at the inventiveness. The lesbian love triangle, the transvestite doctor, the deformed child did not shock me. Nor am I shocked that Barnes is championed for her 'feminism' while her racism is conveniently ignored.<br /><br />I'm just one more flawed reader hoping the reading gods do not judge me any more than I judge an author (in time), and bring me back, as they like to do, with words like this from Djuna Barnes:<br /><br /><i>In time everything is possible and in space everything forgivable; life is but an intermediary vice. <b>There is eternity to blush.</b></i><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18172522383228017791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1289451862" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1289451862" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1289451862?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1289451862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8409350489621128153">1.5* rounded up.<br/><br/><i>Matthew,' she said, 'have you ever loved someone and it became yourself?'<br/>For a moment he did not answer. Taking up the decanter he held it to the light.<br/>'Robin can go anywhere, do anything,' Nora continued, 'because she forgets, and I nowhere because I remember.' She came toward him. 'Matthew,' she said, 'you think I have always been like this. Once I was remorseless, but this is another love — it goes everywhere; there is no place for it to stop — it rots me away.”</i><br/> <br/>I honestly fee</span>
  <span id="freeText8409350489621128153" style="display:none">1.5* rounded up.<br><br><i>Matthew,' she said, 'have you ever loved someone and it became yourself?'<br>For a moment he did not answer. Taking up the decanter he held it to the light.<br>'Robin can go anywhere, do anything,' Nora continued, 'because she forgets, and I nowhere because I remember.' She came toward him. 'Matthew,' she said, 'you think I have always been like this. Once I was remorseless, but this is another love — it goes everywhere; there is no place for it to stop — it rots me away.”</i><br> <br>I honestly feel that I have failed this book.<br> <br>Nightwood was a slight volume but I found it really hard to finish. Some of the language is intriguing but at times trying too hard and coming across as pretentious - in the end I can't make up my mind what which aspect of the book I liked enough to finish it.<br> <br>There was only one character (Nora) that drew my attention and it looks like that character is being ridiculed - though I'm not sure whether the ridicule is an expression of Barnes' attitude or an expression of Barnes' view of society's contempt for that character.<br>I'm sure I'll get my head around it at some point.<br> <br>In short, Nightwood is the story of a cast of outsiders in which Nora and Robin take centre stage. Nora falls in love with Robin, but Robin is too wrapped up in her own desires to see or care about the trail of destruction she leaves behind her.<br> <br>According to Wikipedia (and therefore absolutely reliably true, probably, or not) the character of Robin was based on this lady: <br> <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1435596338p8/1202697.jpg" width="220" height="310" alt="Elsa von Freytag-Loringhoven" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>Nora's character - again according to unreliable sources - seems to reflect Barnes in many ways. I have no idea whether there is an actual autobiographical connection, but having finished the book I don't care to find out.<br> <br>This was a bleak rather bitter read full of pretentious and generalised statements such as:<br> <br><i>"The heart of the jealous knows the best and most satisfying love, that of the other's bed, where the rival perfects the lover's imperfections."</i><br> <br>or<br> <br><i>"Sleep demands of us a guilty immunity. There is not one of us who, given an eternal incognito, a thumbprint nowhere set against our souls, would not commit rape, murder and all abominations."</i><br> <br>And yet, the funny thing about Nightwood is that I'm still intrigued by the book because I couldn't help comparing it to Night Watch by Sarah Waters (of which I am a huge fan). The character I loved most in Night Watch is one of that could easily have been in Nightwood but where Waters likes her characters and celebrates gallantry, Barnes seemed to treat everything and everyone with disdain. <br> <br>So, where Waters character expresses her view on life like this:<br> <br><i>"Someone once said a happy ending depends on where you decide to stop your story. Then again, it could be when you realise your story is not yet over; that you are only at the end of the beginning."</i> (Kay in Night Watch)<br> <br>Barnes' character's outlook is far bleaker:<br> <br><i>“My war brought me many things; let yours bring you as much. Life is not to be told, call it as loud as you like, it will not tell itself. No one will be much or little except in someone else's mind, so be careful of the minds you get into, and remember Lady Macbeth, who had her mind in her hand. We can't all be as safe as that.”</i><br> <br>However, it is not just the bleak outlook that spoilt the book for me. The book starts off introducing the characters and what comes across right from the beginning is that they are all outcasts from society - some because of their physical attributes, some because of their gender, identity, or religion. Rather than to explore these differences, Barnes only emphasizes the stereotypical views held by society - but she does it in a way that seems to be at odds with the story and that seems to define and mock the character of her characters. It is never clear - or it wasn't to me - if this was meant to be irony or just Barnes being bitter and spiteful. It's a fine line and I could not make it out. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8409350489621128153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1365433941" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1365433941" />
    <a title="Kirstine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine"><img alt="Kirstine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505503603p2/5172557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1365433941?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirstine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirstine" href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine">Kirstine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571322862" href="/book/show/25320279-nightwood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=r-2016">r-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=gender-studies">gender-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=lgbtqia">lgbtqia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=1-women">1-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1365433941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11171183880854521826">“<em>Everyman dies finally of that poison known as the-heart-in-the-mouth. Yours is in your hand. Put it back. The eater of it will get a taste for you; in the end his muzzle will be heard barking among your ribs.</em>”<br/><br/>I wish I could say something clever about this book. I’ve put it off till now because I’m at a loss, as I so often am. Some novels force the breath out of your lungs, they force you to breathe the air they breathe, to live the life they create for you and to believe in the things they tell</span>
  <span id="freeText11171183880854521826" style="display:none">“<em>Everyman dies finally of that poison known as the-heart-in-the-mouth. Yours is in your hand. Put it back. The eater of it will get a taste for you; in the end his muzzle will be heard barking among your ribs.</em>”<br /><br />I wish I could say something clever about this book. I’ve put it off till now because I’m at a loss, as I so often am. Some novels force the breath out of your lungs, they force you to breathe the air they breathe, to live the life they create for you and to believe in the things they tell you. When done you forget your own words, you’re left with an empty spot where your language used to be. I didn’t know what to say. I still don’t, which is a compliment, truly, to the power of this novel. <br /><br />There’s an obsessiveness to it, a desperate desire that you know cannot be fulfilled. Only the doctor of the story, a cross dresser, who perhaps inhabits more than simply one world, perhaps all the worlds, a sort of prophet, who spills truths as if they were drops of water, seems consigned to this fate of unfulfillment. <br /><br />At the center is Robin, a child, a lover, an apparition, she slips through the hands of everyone, even those who loves her desperately. And Nora, the doomed lover, helplessly waiting for Robin to return, to settle down, knowing it’s not in her nature. <br /><br />It’s a condensed story, weaving several fates together, always intersecting, touching each other even after the last goodbye has been said. The characters are not many, but they contain multitudes, worlds, they are so many things on so few pages. <br /><br />I know you shouldn’t compare, but I read Ulysses last January, and I read Nightwood this January, and they are alike. In style, in tone, in vision, and in so many ways they are opposites. Perhaps Nightwood is what would’ve happened had Molly Bloom left her dull husband and had an affair with a woman. And this theme of lesbianism is crucial, because it gives voice to those who didn’t have one, and gives them a place in modern, classic literature. I’m saddened Nightwood is not a more widely known work (or so it seems to me), because it deserves attention, scrutiny, criticism and devoted love. <br /><br />It’s love, obsession, joy, jealousy, sexuality, wonder, desperation, all the big things in a constantly transforming pattern. You give yourself up to the novel, it gives itself back to you. <br /><br />"<em>Our bones ache only while the flesh is on them. Stretch it as thin as the temple flesh of an ailing woman and still it serves to ache the bone and to move the bone about; and in like manner the night is skin pulled over the head of the day that the day may be in torment. We will find no comfort until the night melts away; until the fury of the night rots out its fire.</em>"</span>
  <a data-text-id="11171183880854521826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1763761295" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1763761295" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1763761295?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571322862" href="/book/show/25320279-nightwood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1763761295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6810868660184910154">I enjoyed the style and originality of <i>Nightwood</i>, but didn't love it, for two reasons. The first is that it is very much of its time. The novel feels like a push-back, a response to the status-quo, an attempt to embody some form of modernity. I felt I lacked context; I found it difficult to meaningfully relate to this narrow, obsolete zeitgeist. The second reason, is that I could not connect deeply enough to the characters - especially to the three women - to feel involved in their minds and the</span>
  <span id="freeText6810868660184910154" style="display:none">I enjoyed the style and originality of <i>Nightwood</i>, but didn't love it, for two reasons. The first is that it is very much of its time. The novel feels like a push-back, a response to the status-quo, an attempt to embody some form of modernity. I felt I lacked context; I found it difficult to meaningfully relate to this narrow, obsolete zeitgeist. The second reason, is that I could not connect deeply enough to the characters - especially to the three women - to feel involved in their minds and the inner workings of their relationships. Or perhaps I connected sufficiently, but just wasn't moved. I would say this is a consequence of the distance created by the writing style. T.S. Eliot says in the preface that the novel needs to be read twice to be appreciated. Maybe, then, the problem is that I read <i>Nightwood</i> only once, or maybe the characters just weren't sufficiently well expressed. This is an influential novel, but it feels to me frozen in time, and no longer so essential.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6810868660184910154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1141096532" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1141096532" />
    <a title="Bloodorange" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange"><img alt="Bloodorange" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386872100p2/23066833.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1141096532?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bloodorange" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bloodorange" href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange">Bloodorange</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571209286" href="/book/show/894658.Nightwood">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=year-of-reading-women-2015">year-of-reading-women-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=modernism">modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=us">us</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1141096532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10158549590626031177">First star for the disappointment. Second - for the use of the language, the cadence of sentences, and the use of punctuation, semicolon in particular. I think I'll start showing fragments of <i>Nighwood</i> to my students, who mostly only believe in commas, to show them how punctuation adds clarity and tension.<blockquote>He was usually seen walking or driving alone, dressed as if expecting to participate in some great event, though there was no function in the world for which he could be said to be properly garb</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10158549590626031177" style="display:none">First star for the disappointment. Second - for the use of the language, the cadence of sentences, and the use of punctuation, semicolon in particular. I think I'll start showing fragments of <i>Nighwood</i> to my students, who mostly only believe in commas, to show them how punctuation adds clarity and tension.<blockquote>He was usually seen walking or driving alone, dressed as if expecting to participate in some great event, though there was no function in the world for which he could be said to be properly garbed; wishing to be correct at any moment, he was tailored in part for the evening and in part for the day.</blockquote>Consider this gem, an extract form the first chapter:<br /><blockquote>Three massive pianos (Hedvig had played the waltzes of her time with the masterly stroke of a man, in the tempo of her blood, rapid and rising - that quick mannerliness of touch associated with the playing of the Viennese, who, though pricked with the love of rhythm, execute its demands in the duelling manner) sprawled over the thick dragon's-blood pile of rugs from Madrid. The study harboured two rambling desks in rich and bloody wood. Hedvig had liked things in twos and threes. Into the middle arch of each desk silver-headed brads had been hammered to form a lion, a bear, a ram, a dove and in their midst a flaming torch. The design was executed under the supervision of Guido who, thinking on the instant, claimed it as the Volkbein field, though it turned out to be a bit of heraldry long since in decline beneath the papal frown. The full length windows (a French touch that Guido thought handsome) overlooking the park were curtained in native velvets and stuffs from Tunis and the Venetian blinds were of that peculiarly sombre shade of red so loved by the Austrians. Against the panels of oak that reared themselves above the long table and up to the curving ceiling hung life-sized portraits of Guido's claim to father and mother. The lady was a sumptuous Florentine with bright sly eyes and overt mouth. Great puffed and pearled sleeves rose to the pricked-eared pointings of the stiff lace about the head, conical and braided. The deep accumulation of dress fell about her in groined shadows, the train, rambling through a vista of primitive trees, was carpet-thick. She seemed to be expecting a bird.</blockquote>Before long, however, it degenerates into this:<blockquote>'Love is death, come upon with passion; I know that is why love is wisdom.I love her as one condemned to it.' <br />'Oh, Widow Lazarus! Arisen from your dead! Oh, lunatic humour of the moon! Behold this fearful tree, on which sits singing the drearful bird – Turdus musicus‚ or European singing thrush; sitting and singing the refrain-all in the tear-wet night — and it starts out large, but it ends like I Hear You Calling Me, or Kiss Me Again, gone wild...."</blockquote>Modernist preoccupations with social classes, appearances, outsiders, loneliness rendered in Belle Epoque language do not work: formal aspects of modernism I so loved in, say, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, irritate me here; the language - though I loved and still respect Wilde, Huysmans, and (later) Firbank - is pointlessly excessive. I forced myself to finish this book. Started and finished thanks to 2015: The Year of Reading Women: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/152644-2015-the-year-of-reading-women">https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10158549590626031177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17759306966041752059">"I have been loved," she said, "By something strange, and it has forgotten me!"<br/><br/>Here is something strange, a 1936 landmark of modernist gay poetry disguised as a novella. It's written with unstoppable confidence and verve and the dangerous knowledge that all people are possible, and any of them may be geniuses. It follows a love triangle of three women as they pursue each other hopelessly around the world. A gender-fluid doctor provides commentary and impenetrable advice. "I have a narrative," he</span>
  <span id="freeText17759306966041752059" style="display:none">"I have been loved," she said, "By something strange, and it has forgotten me!"<br /><br />Here is something strange, a 1936 landmark of modernist gay poetry disguised as a novella. It's written with unstoppable confidence and verve and the dangerous knowledge that all people are possible, and any of them may be geniuses. It follows a love triangle of three women as they pursue each other hopelessly around the world. A gender-fluid doctor provides commentary and impenetrable advice. "I have a narrative," he says, "but you will be put to it to find it." And you're like yeah, that's what people say right before they ramble for like 20 pages, you're not fooling anyone doc. This book is beautiful but I didn't understand most of it.<br /><br />Robin Vote, who "was always holding God's bag of tricks upside down," comes in for the worst of it. Her true love Nora Flood (these are great names) abandons her for some lisping widow, described hilariously: <br /><blockquote>The words that fell from her mouth seemed to have been lent to her; had she been forced to invent a vocabulary for herself, it would have been a vocabulary of two words, "ah" and "oh."</blockquote><br />Nora is a tortured disaster: "Every hour is my last, and," she said desperately, "one can't live one's last hour all one's life!"<br /><br />And "Can't you be quiet now?" the doctor said. "Can't you be done now, can't you give up? Now be still, now that you know what the world is about, knowing it's about nothing?"<br /><br />The answer is no.</span>

<div id="review_178788716" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178788716" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178788716?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781564780805" href="/book/show/53103.Nightwood">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">moth-flame loves undying and inescapable</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      The terrifying body language of a doomed couple glimpsed across the room
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=interwar-maladies">interwar-maladies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178788716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11781808867233098911">A strange and oddly removed portrayal of chained relationship collapse. Or not so much collapse, as the structures seem never so well-built as to merit the power and finality of a "collapse". Instead, constructed of ephemeral and ill-defined desires, these relationships barely exist to begin with, already well into their inexorable fade into nonexistence. The strongest structures about them were always the bitter unflagging despair of a human connection that will never, never be found. Even when</span>
  <span id="freeText11781808867233098911" style="display:none">A strange and oddly removed portrayal of chained relationship collapse. Or not so much collapse, as the structures seem never so well-built as to merit the power and finality of a "collapse". Instead, constructed of ephemeral and ill-defined desires, these relationships barely exist to begin with, already well into their inexorable fade into nonexistence. The strongest structures about them were always the bitter unflagging despair of a human connection that will never, never be found. Even when we closely follow the characters, though, there's a distance (perhaps symptomatic of their ineffective bonds), as if watching them from across a room or hearing about their movements only via gossip and hearsay (as their vague society-figure roles assure, I suppose). Fitting then that the most impassioned and immediate bits are soliloquies from a great observer and gossip, an extraordinary figure in the modernism of the earlier 20th century, a tragic cross-dressing unlicensed doctor who seems to always be on hand for the key action, and ready to offer some manner of baroquely-wrought interpretation, though usually of such flying grandiosity and peculiarity as to demand close scrutiny and interpretation of its own. At first I thought him a bore, intermittently throughout I thought him incomprehensible (if he is truly Barnes' mouthpiece, let's say "very personal"), by the end, I found him marvellous.<br /><br />Still, though, the distance. What does Robin really, really want? We can see her only through others, is this the only we we can see anyone, second hand even if only through the intermediate of our own uncontrolled interiors and emotions? And what does Nora want? Really, why is it Robin to whom she gives such destroying power? And Jenny, what really is Jenny's significant completion of this dire trinity? We're told exactly what Felix wants, I suppose, but Felix is rather the least interesting of the bunch (but wait, is his knowability his lack of interest? A seductive notion, but no, I feel I didn't even know Nora well enough for this level of intrigue to take hold.)<br /><br />Finally, the ending: against the second-hand action of the rest of the novel, it burns in a devastating, immaculately sullied immediacy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11781808867233098911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_843254869" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/843254869" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/843254869?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=grsm-lgbtqia">grsm-lgbtqia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=2015year-reading-women">2015year-reading-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=inter-war">inter-war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=500gbw">500gbw</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=bechdel-pass">bechdel-pass</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer843254869" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5754213843450800491">I'm evidently just not brilliantly smart enough to enjoy this book as I couldn't see the point of it at all. In a way it reminded me of Shakespeare, extemporising on themes of love, sexual jealousy and personality in flights of poetry. But remember why Shakespeare is a little bit obscure and difficult, because we hear it through the long shadows of the centuries; a couple more, I read, and we'll have to translate it, like Chaucer. Nightwood is dense and difficult at eighty, presumably because it</span>
  <span id="freeText5754213843450800491" style="display:none">I'm evidently just not brilliantly smart enough to enjoy this book as I couldn't see the point of it at all. In a way it reminded me of Shakespeare, extemporising on themes of love, sexual jealousy and personality in flights of poetry. But remember why Shakespeare is a little bit obscure and difficult, because we hear it through the long shadows of the centuries; a couple more, I read, and we'll have to translate it, like Chaucer. Nightwood is dense and difficult at eighty, presumably because it wasn't written for the peanut-crunching crowd. And I don't have a problem with dense and difficult per se, but for whatever reason I didn't get a lot out of it here.<br /><br />T S Eliot reverentially introduces the text and warns us it's not a philosophical treatise but since it did nothing for me as poem, what's left? Am I supposed to ignore the blows of the hammer with which Barnes nails the world and its peoples to their definitions, to history and racialisation? Don't tell me whites haven't always known not to use the n-word, don't tell me Barnes is speaking in sympathy when when she calls it inadvisable to give birth to a Jew. I know, no ill intended, and I'm not appeased.<br /><br />Unlike Shakespeare and his sister Virginia Woolf, who open the world like a magic box and run its distinctions together like oil paints, Barnes pins down the rising spirits she caught dancing to the strange music of a new century and turns them back into dust and metal. Gender might have broken in her hand like an egg but inside it is the same old misery.<br /><br />Here love redeems nothing and no one, all life rots on the vine of poisoned Europe, which may well be the truth of the day, for afterwards Europe convulsed in a sickness and swallowed its children again, but while it may be truth it's not life Eliot, don't tell me this is life, it's only music, and I've heard enough European dirges.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5754213843450800491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1762478356" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1762478356" />
    <a title="Xandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6600821-xandra"><img alt="Xandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587055091p2/6600821.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1762478356?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xandra" href="/user/show/6600821-xandra">Xandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=lesbian-bi">lesbian-bi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=1900-1949">1900-1949</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=by-women">by-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=gender">gender</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1762478356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2218572971991478547">This definitely feels like an Important Book. You read it and you think "I’ve haven’t read anything like it before". So part of your enjoyment comes from the element of novelty. And it’s a difficult novel, which automatically makes it exciting. But there are difficult novels that are stimulating and rich in ideas, and difficult novels that weigh the reader down. Instead of lifting you up on a happy cloud of fulfilment, they wear you out. For me, <i>Nightwood</i> falls into the latter category. It has m</span>
  <span id="freeText2218572971991478547" style="display:none">This definitely feels like an Important Book. You read it and you think "I’ve haven’t read anything like it before". So part of your enjoyment comes from the element of novelty. And it’s a difficult novel, which automatically makes it exciting. But there are difficult novels that are stimulating and rich in ideas, and difficult novels that weigh the reader down. Instead of lifting you up on a happy cloud of fulfilment, they wear you out. For me, <i>Nightwood</i> falls into the latter category. It has many good parts, but The Part, the one that I felt was the focus of the book and should have been the most significant, didn’t have a great impact on me. I struggled for two days with that cumbersome monologue and I’m sure a lot of it went straight over my head. Even so, it was a worthy read and the ending is so bizarre it almost makes you want to go back to the beginning and read the book again.</span>
