    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_784830119" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/784830119" />
    <a title="Sentimental Surrealist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9707921-sentimental-surrealist"><img alt="Sentimental Surrealist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412974239p2/9707921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/784830119?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sentimental Surrealist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sentimental Surrealist" href="/user/show/9707921-sentimental-surrealist">Sentimental Surrealist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer784830119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13766359323347734638">A book preceded by and shrouded in its reputation for difficulty, which for me translates to its breaking of a long-established and yet mostly unwritten fictional rule: you do not make the bulk of your novel a series of conversations, especially if those conversations are about the characters’ worldviews. Most of this rule, as I see it, was born out of the good old “who-do-you-think-you’re-fooling” principle - the idea is if you’re not too careful with such a novel, you’ve got something that loo</span>
  <span id="freeText13766359323347734638" style="display:none">A book preceded by and shrouded in its reputation for difficulty, which for me translates to its breaking of a long-established and yet mostly unwritten fictional rule: you do not make the bulk of your novel a series of conversations, especially if those conversations are about the characters’ worldviews. Most of this rule, as I see it, was born out of the good old “who-do-you-think-you’re-fooling” principle - the idea is if you’re not too careful with such a novel, you’ve got something that looks, talks, and quacks a lot more like a philosophical treatise than the type of storytelling we’re used to in the twentieth century.<br /><br />“Well, quack quack, motherfucker,” says Barnes, temporarily turning into an action hero before reverting to her preferred and frankly much cooler stance as prophet/philosopher/poet of our oft-confused emotional and erotic states. “I’m going to write this one this way, and you’ll like it so much you’ll try to visit my Greenwich apartment. But don’t think you’ll meet me or anything, because I’ll yell at you to leave me alone.” Well, not everyone in this world craves attention, and maybe not everyone who wanted to got the chance to shoot the shit with Djuna about her creative process or views of gender or anything of the sort, but she left this for us and for that we should all be happy.<br /><br />So let’s get into Nightwood, which is one of the greatest books to ever do everything a book shouldn’t. You know how people tend to complain about novels that seem like seriously extended short stories? That’s sort of where Nightwood’s at. Most of the main plot takes place in two middle chapters, which are surrounded by the much-discussed serious discourse where the author outlines views of gender, sexuality, love and life. This is another one of those Things You Should Not Do, and I’ve raked a lot of authors over the coals for doing that. How does Barnes get away with it? <br /><br />Well, T.S. Eliot says in his famous intro that we believe Robin is real without knowing why. I’ll see Eliot but raise him one: all of these characters seem real, and in their realness become tragic. It’s through the discourse that we get their realness and their tragedy in its fullness - Dr. O’Connor’s unheeded wisdom and cosmic isolation, Nora’s willingness to fly off a cliff for what she believes, Robin’s lack of awareness about what she really wants. These are real characters, characters that you might be able to point to equivalents of in your day-to-day lives. So when Nora and O’Connor or Felix and O’Connor talk, it feels like real conversation and not a veil for Barnes’ philosophy. <br /><br />Plus the prose. My god, the prose. Since this novel’s so dialog-heavy, Barnes doesn’t have a whole world of opportunities to exercise that particular muscle, but when she hits on it, my fucking <i>god</i>. Check out the brilliant combination of motion, emotion and eroticism in the book’s final chapter, which just might split your heart in two and reduce you to tears. Or the rush of Nora and Robin falling for each other, where she puts you so much into the moment it’s like you’re falling in love with both of them. Which I guess goes back to the fact that Barnes writes good characters. Amazing what good characters can do for a book, isn’t it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="13766359323347734638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zYqCWtErigjqhFpd+dpsOqoQyaZ6nQyHN6uQdzfg5t7CDggM+xdjt/aIDI+xRtPUyRtm9fqzC5cK77f50ZZrHQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xMxl1bfe1HSDhwSWqRVDJPDR7YbZmjPzE8f+ujMrl2vLSO+DneI9y5+LUkThifzKk9pC1Vm0NOMug9k01V0aqA==')})
  }

</script>

Lucrécia Neves is ready to marry. Her suitors—soldierly Felipe, pensive Perseu, dependable Mateus—are attracted to her tawdry not-quite-beauty, which is of a piece with Sao Geraldo, the rough-and-ready township she inhabits. Civilization is on its way to this place, where wild horses still roam. As Lucrécia is tamed by marriage, Sao Geraldo gradually expels its horses; and as the town strives for the highest attainment it can conceive—a viaduct—it takes on the progressively more metropolitan manners that Lucrécia, with her vulgar ambitions, desires too. Yet it is precisely through this woman’s superficiality—her identification with the porcelain knickknacks in her mother’s parlor—that Clarice Lispector creates a profound and enigmatic meditation on “the mystery of the thing.” Written in Europe shortly after Clarice Lispector’s own marriage, The Besieged City is a proving ground for the intricate language and the radical ideas that characterize In America she has been posthumously launched as the electric-age Blake, she has been translated into French and Italian to great acclaim, and in the Times Literary Supplement Thom Gunn compared her to the great Augustan satirists. Her reclamation as an English poet is long overdue.\n\nPound, Moore and Williams valued her work, while British critics openly scorned it. Not only were her futurist techniques unlike anything they had encountered before, but her subjects -- procreation, parturition, prostitution, suicide, addiction, retardation -- were considered shocking even by some modernists.\n\nShe vanished from the literary scene just as dramatically as she had arrived on it, and for much of the century her bold experiments remained a well-kept secret. Carcanet first introduced her work to British readers in 1985 in Roger Conover\'s The Last Lunar Baedeker, a collected writings. This new edition updates our earlier volume and presents more reliable texts of the essential Loy poems. It includes more extensive notes and apparatus, and features a number of previously unknown works rescued from Dada archives and obscure avant-garde little magazines. All of Loy\'s canonical Futurist and feminist satires are included, as are the celebrated poems from her Paris and New York periods, the complete cycle of \`Love Songs\', and her famous portraits-in-verse which define the trajectory of her favoured company and geography -- from fellow modernists Joyce and Brancusi in Paris in the 1920s to fellow destitutes in New York\'s Lower East Revealing that side of life which is never seen by the waking eye but which dreams and drugs can suddenly emphasize, this startling discovery illustrates how these nighttime illuminations reveal the       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/30013.Djuna_Barnes">About Djuna Barnes</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/30013.Djuna_Barnes"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1212886093p3/30013.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/30013.Djuna_Barnes">Djuna Barnes
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
354
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1875637703396633583">Barnes has been cited as an influence by writers as diverse as Truman Capote, William Goyen, Isak Dinesen, John Hawkes, Bertha Harris and Anaïs Nin. Writer Bertha Harris described her work as "practically the only available expression of lesbian culture we have in the modern western world" since Sappho.<br/><br/>Barnes played an important part in the development of 20th century English language modernist wr</span>
  <span id="freeText1875637703396633583" style="display:none">Barnes has been cited as an influence by writers as diverse as Truman Capote, William Goyen, Isak Dinesen, John Hawkes, Bertha Harris and Anaïs Nin. Writer Bertha Harris described her work as "practically the only available expression of lesbian culture we have in the modern western world" since Sappho.<br /><br />Barnes played an important part in the development of 20th century English language modernist writing and was one of the key figures in 1920s and 30s bohemian Paris after filling a similar role in the Greenwich Village of the teens. Her novel Nightwood became a cult work of modern fiction, helped by an introduction by T. S. Eliot. It stands out today for its portrayal of lesbian themes and its distinctive writing style. Since Barnes's death, interest in her work has grown and many of her books are back in print.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1875637703396633583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
